Thatâ€™s always the problem with solar, you need to jack up your batteries, another thing is to introduce mass in your house, if you build with concrete during the day when you have a ton of extra energy you just crank up your air and get it nice and cool and have a large amount of thermal mass is there. It will absorb heat when itâ€™s hot and dissipate heat when itâ€™s cold, thermal mass is a critical part of building green, But the easy option is just increase your battery power yeah so you just have plenty of power when the sun is down.  A lot of people will just live with the temperatures and all of that but I love green power but itâ€™s not very efficient or practical so if I was ever to do it I would way overdo it and just have a ton of battery. I would have three days worth of energy saved up and enough panels to charge it completely in one day with a moderate amount of sun. Itâ€™s possible in the winter you might have to throw in a generator if itâ€™s really dark and everything is covered with snow but it all just comes down to money, Sollers doable itâ€™s just not a very economical option.  Other options would be to get a geothermal system but for practical applications for you guys I would say thermal mass would be good if you had more room but the easy solution is just going to be more battery.  The other option is to run your house cooler during the day, I use spray foam if itâ€™s done correctly you should have a fairly efficient system, maybe improving windows insulation once itâ€™s cold in there it shouldnâ€™t take nearly as much to keep going. An air conditioning guy taught me that the worst thing you can do is leave your conditioner off all day long and then come home and turn it on because it works five times itâ€™s hard to cool your house as it does to maintain your house being cool so you just want to keep your house cool where itâ€™s comfortable and you want it and that your most efficient option other than to just suffer with hot all the time.    I agree with you on not mixing batteries but you can if you segment your usage. Put in a separate circuit for heating and cooling and just put the new battery on that and let the old batteries run for another 25 years doing your lights and other electric stuff, basically have two separate solar systems, itâ€™s no different than adding a second panel at your house like having one for the garage and one for the main house.

Batteries:  1) They donâ€™t like heat.  Heat kills batteries.  You have incredible heat.  In someplace like the Pacific Northwest, batteries will last 5 years.  In the Sandhills in North Carolina, 4 years.  In Gulf Coast Alabama/Mississippi and Louisiana and East Texasâ€¦â€¦4 years is stretching the lifespan of the batteries live in the heat.  2) When you test batteries and they show 80-85% of â€œcapabilityâ€ - theyâ€™re close to dead.  You get close to 80% and the batteries will DIE.  Given that you have a lot of power during the day (when the sun is hitting your solar panels) and little power at dawnâ€¦â€¦your batteries are borderline useless.
Great job with the skirting. A finishing touch that adds so much character to your house. Stay safe.
You've got an awesome channel and I've throughly enjoyed watching y'all build your home. May, June, and July were the hottest months on record here in CenTex, with virtually no rainfall. We've seen some in the last couple of weeks, and the temps have gone down, so some relief is in sight! You are right on: If you are running net zero for electricity usage to start with (your solar generation produces 100% of your power needs), your battery storage should be about 50% of your daily maximum utilization in the summer to ensure your batteries will last through the night with a constant A/C running. This does not account for the excessive power use during really cold winter nights, but I think it's easier to put clothes on to keep warm when that happens. Heat pumps with auxillary electric heating elements consume a massive amount of power (much more than A/C). My property now is net zero: We produce about 26MWh per year, with plans to expand that to 36 MWh/yr in 2023, because EVs consume a lot of electricity. Propane/natural gas is so much more efficient for heating. I didn't build my house, but I would have installed propane lines if I had built it. For water I've learned: Have about 40-50% of storage capacity for the average annual total rainwater collection capacity of your system. With good water usage practices, it should allow for full collection ability while not drawing down your tanks to empty - and have a buffer. Our rainwater collection system currently has 5000 gallons of capacity, but it is nowhere near enough to service our home without water interruptions. So it will be expanding to 10000 gallons next year too. By the end of 2023, we will be able to go fully off grid if we want to. If not, we'll at least be ready for the next Texas snowpocalypse. Thanks for sharing yourselves with us. All the best to your budding family. ğŸ™‚

Oversized is fine but there still may be issues that arise. You should be thinking critically. Double and triple redundancies. Along with increasing your storage capacity you should think about adding wind generation for those cloudy but windy days. If you have an adequate water source like a decent flowing stream on your property, you could look into microhydro systems. And more importantly, adding a full size back up generator should be essential. Adding propane to run a back up generator as well as emergency heat in the winter would be a great asset and with Texas often in the path of many hurricanes and tornados, it would seem to be a no-brainer. This is my opinion only, but despite all of the water recovery systems in place, I believe it is critical to have a good deep well on any property. Great conversation about the real life issues with living with off grid systems. Re your set up. look into a duel power element ( AC one side and DC the other) for your water heater and add a couple of solar panels specifically for it use, as you can direct DC heat water in a water cylinder via a duel element. Also look at water cylinder kits that connect to you AirCon heat exchangers. As exchanging heat for the AirCon into or out of your water cylinder, reduces the amount of work your AirCon will have to do on extremely hot days, and thus it'll use less power. Mix Energy in the UK (not affiliated) have product, but more importantly have great documentation that speaks to this point. You'll probably want to source USA based products if you go that route. I hope that helps D

You guys, if the heat breaks at night, turn OFF your air-conditioning and instead just get fans and blow in the cold air through every window...then close the house back up and tip-shut the blinds to keep the sun from beating in before the heat re-cranks every morning. You guys should really check your comments sections. ğŸ˜‰Back when yall were installing the mini splits, I commented on you having 3 separate 20amp outdoor condensers would put too much load on the electrical. 3 condensers means 3 separate motors to turn 3 compressors full of freon. That's triple the A/C load on a house that's less than 1000sqft. A simple 3 ton 3 zone 12k x 12k x 12k mini split with single condenser (placed on the east side of the house away from the evening sun + lengthening the lines if needed) would have frozen you guys in that little house. I have a 2.5 ton 3 zone on my 800sqft house for 8yrs in west Texas. The only reason to have separate units like that is if you want to totally cool and heat separate areas of the home simultaneously.  Edit: Spenser, would love to know what your current draw is on those 3 units at night with all the lights out? Could you report that to us?

Many of you have mentioned a well which sounds easy and logical.  But one thing I discovered when I was looking at the idea of using a gasoline generator to power a house independent of the grid was that many deep well pumps use a LOT of wattage/amperage upon startup with a bit less during the repressurization of the water in the system/pressure tank.  This MIGHT be the reason why they are not digging a well (due to available stored electricity).  That being said, maybe their well wouldn't need to be a deep well (greater than 50') so what I said above may not apply.

One of my pet peeves is when someone uses the term "footage". It is never used in electronic recording and i jave been recording video since videotape was invented. The term "Footage" is used by people who use film as thier medium, as film is measured in feet. A typical film magazine will hold 400 feet of film. This is untrue in the video recording world. We dont measure footage of video tape. We use time to measure. Either by time code or by mechanical tape counter calibrated to time & tape speed. Now using digital we still use time code to locate "clips" to assembled into a scene. Please just call them clips & not footage, as footage has no meaning

I have an off grid tiny house(40Ã—14) in SW Florida, powered by solar . 5kw solar aray,  2Ã— 3kw inverters running in parallel,  30kw lithium battery . All the equipment is in separate shed, 70 feet away from house. Hey yall !  Your love has built this house and nothing else.  God bless you and your children.
I've been watching you all from the beginning almost.  Solar Cell / battery is always supplemental electricity. It keeps you from using too much of grid power. Sufficient number of batteries is important, plus the condition of the batteries, which you've found out of course. As soon as you can, I'd advise getting Coop power routed to your place. I use electrical fans on pedestals. I love my fans, too. You just have to raise your thermostat some while using the fans. Water, that is always an issue.  My wife is from the Philippines and the house she had built in the Philippines, she has a tower tank for extra water. It was a bit huge, but it was great until they had city water piped in.  Water catching is so important when living off the grid. Plastic tanks used to help catch the water from your roof.  Using dish water to flush the toilet helps some. This one guy that lives out in the desert part of West Texas has three or four plastic tanks.  He manages but it takes planning.  In the summertime, forget the grass. It always comes back anyway.  Having an extra set of batteries when your A/C drains the set it's using, it can switch to the second set. Still, on cloudy rainy days, you're going to have a few down days. Another choice is that gas generator as a backup. They look like a large condenser unit. I hear they are quiet but don't get me to lying.  Okay, enough written here. Hope it helps a little.

Your solar system has already cost more energy than a 4 person family uses in 5 years on grid, and you haven't even changed your batteries, yet. One thing that occurs during summer on a structure is called often thermal soak-storage and it gets released as the outside  temperature falls at night. Essentially a building holds heat like a radiator and when the inside temperature is greater than the outside around it sheds that heat almost like you've kicked on the furnace. Though its likely their home isn't as bad as those made of brick when it comes to the effect, it can be mitigated some what by venting the heat that it will build during the day. If the windows on the center of the house above their head can be opened it will draft the heat out like a chimney and its why roofs often have attic vents or even fans to move it out. What makes their container home energy efficient when it come to insulation also makes it an oven as well during the summer and venting the passive heat it acquires during they day will help as well.

This is what it looks like when there is 420 ppm CO2 in the atmosphere.. When I was a kid, CO2 was 350ppm... 2  ppm annual increasing CO2 is why you continue to see "daily record high temperature"...  Yes, that's what 2 ppm per year (6,000 Olympic sized swimming pools of oil burned into the atmosphere per day) will do...  Our best hope would be to figure out Fusion power, then figure out how to use that power to bottle up Millions of years worth of fossil fuel that we have burned in the span of a century...  So the icecaps are melting 6 times faster than the original computer models accounted for.  One reason is that forest fires create global soot, which as it lands on polar ice, makes that ice blacken, thus creating less reflectivity, causing it to melt faster than pristine ice.  So, then we are witnessing an "avalanche effect" where these systems start to self amplify... More heat causes more fires, causes more soot, causes more melting, causes more heat, causes less rain...  We are not going to be able to "move away" from this problem.. It will follow us.  I say take every penny we are spending on "going to Mars or the Moon", and instead figure out 1) Fusion, then 2) How to geoengineer the gas composition of our atmosphere... Wait!
The house is looking great! At this stage, shade-producing greenery is gonna be your best friend. Planting leafy trees on the south side of the property will go a long way to bringing your heat gain down in summer (then dropping those leaves in fall and giving you heat gain in winter). Tall evergreens closer to the house won't hurt, either. You might consider making a plan to regularly add batteries to your cache, a few at a time, to increase your storage (and make sure that battery bank has shading, too!).   I'm in the Washington, DC area, where we regularly have high-90s and high humidity, this year we've broken into the mid-hundreds "feels like" temps on multiple weeks... but the tree shading we've arranged around the house (starting years ago) help keep our place comfortable. Our backyard is surrounded by trees, and it's shady year-round. (Not much of a help right now, but in another 10-20 years, you'll be so happy you have that shade.)
In Cape Town South Africa we were around two weeks to ground zero for Cape town.  We used grey water to fill up our toilet cisterns which came from our washing machine and shower. We then also used grey water for the garden.  Trick with that was to allow the water to settle so soap etc fell to the bottom.  Rinsing dishes was not an option most of the time.  Cooking water from cooking vegetables was kept and used along with grey water.  We washed/showered once a week and washed with a cloth and small bucket of water on the other days. Filled a cup with water to brush our teeth instead of letting tap run. When showering we stood in a large bucket to collect water.  Point is.... It can be done and we did it for almost a year..... Automatic Washing machines use tons of water.  If you want to use less water for washing clothes buy a twin tub.  Fill up machine with water,  put drain hose to drain water back into washing machine tub when you spin.  Wash all clothes first (re-using water everytime from spinning washing) and when finished drain water into a very large container for grey water,  fill tub with fresh water,  rinse with drain hose draining back into tub until you notice that the water needs replacing,  drain for grey water and fill with fresh.... Sorry for long explanation but that is how we survived until we got rain again and the dams filled up.  A practice we followed with the toilets was if it is brown flush it down,  if it is yellow let it mellow lol...

Venomous snakes usually have pupils in the shape of a vertical slit. Non-venomous snakes, on the other hand, have circular pupils. Unfortunately there are exceptions: the cobra, the black mamba, the inland taipan and the coral snake have round pupils, but are very poisonous! So I'm pretty sure your new snake isn't poisonous.

Guys,  Seriously a well with a level switch will make your lives sooooo much easier take your storage and bury it in a berm to keep it from freezing and use the well to keep it at least half full and you never have to worry about it again. As for the batteries yeah not sure any place that gets below freezing or in the 100+ degree heat is your best bet for battery life. Most folks have a room in the house with all the controllers and batteries just for that reason. Worst case, a small building outside with the controllers and batteries with maybe a small cooling unit. The charging of the batteries and maybe a heat lamp bulb should keep a small insulated building above freezing in the winter and the small cooling unit for summer when its really hot out. Power and water are the most important things so build them to last and supply all your needs no matter what the weather does because as you guys have seen it will not cooperate. Im actually drawing up plans for a hydroelectric set up for the cabin on our creek property. If/when I get it done I'll shoot you guys some pics. Keep up the good work. Edit*** Forgot to add you can save those cedar shavings from the planer and they work great for do bedding. It looked like you guys left a place the doggos could get under the porch or some place and if you spread that under there it will help keep bugs out and give them a place to lie. Best thing is that since its an aromatic wood no worries with termites.

Establishment-semi-establishment-comprehensive. It refers to the logical structure of dialectics. Hegel is by far the person who conducted philosophical arguments according to dialectics. Dialectic is not a parallel of three categories with equal status, but a structural characteristic of convergent upwardness in which two opposing categories achieve harmonious unification. In Hegel, dialectic is not only a method of argument, but also a method of existence of the object of argument itself. In other words, the internal structure of the world's fundamental order, "ideology," and the way ideology is revealed as a temporal and spatial reality are dialectical, so ideology and reality form a system, and philosophical arguments that reveal these two dimensional principles must also have dialectical system.  Hegel intends to deal with aesthetics in a completely dialectical system. For him, art, the object of aesthetics, is a form of 'absolute spirit' like religion and philosophy. Absolute spirit refers to the realm of the human mind that recognizes the absolute truth, 'ideology'. Art, religion, and philosophy have the same absolute truth, but are classified according to differences in the form of perception. The forms corresponding to each of the three forms of absolute mind are intuition, representation, and thought. Intuition is an intelligence that sensibly perceives a given material object, representation is an intelligence that recalls the mind from the inside regardless of the presence or absence of a material object, and reason is a pure logical intelligence that identifies the object through the concept. Accordingly, each of the three forms is defined as 'the absolute spirit of intuition', 'the absolute spirit of representation', and 'the absolute spirit of thinking'. According to Hegel, the outwardness of intuition and the innerness of representation are synthesized from thought, and accordingly, the objectivity of art and the subjectivity of religion are synthesized from philosophy.  Differences between formats lead to significant differences in the level of recognition of content. In Hegel, the absolute truth, the content of the absolute spirit, is inherently logical and rational. Since art is intuitive, religion is represented, and philosophy is thought, these three forms are graded step by step. In other words, art is the absolute spirit of the beginner stage, religion is the growth stage, and philosophy is the mature stage. Accordingly, in the order of art-religion-philosophy, the only absolute spirit in name and reality is based on the best intelligence, that is, philosophy, and art can function as an absolute spirit is limited to the far past in which the universal intelligence of mankind has not been developed.  The attraction of dialectics lies in 'comprehensive'. The category of comprehensive should not end with a one-sided victory of one of the two opposing categories, nor should it appear in a neutral state in which the inherent essential regulations of the two categories are extinguished. The synthesis is established by creating the best category that is qualitatively enhanced by the essential regulations of the two.  This is also the excellence of dialectics emphasized by Hegel. Therefore, it was his philosophical plan to solicit a strict and consistent academic system optimized for the principle of dialectic. However, can his achievements be evaluated as completing the plan without any flaws? When it comes to aesthetics, the yes answer will not be easy. The strategy of organizing the form of intelligence in the order of intuition, representation, and thought, and organizing the absolute spirit in the order of art, religion, and philosophy accordingly seems to be a typical composition according to the dialectic model. However, in the process from intuition to thought, externalities are gradually erased and internalities are gradually strengthened and completed, and in the process from art to philosophy, objectivity is gradually erased and subjectivity is gradually strengthened and completed, but true dialectical synthesis is not achieved. The essence of the outwardness of intuition and the objectivity of art is, above all, sensory perception, and these core elements are completely erased at the stage of synthesis he refers to. To be faithful to dialectics, Hegel should have added the absolute spirit of the stage in which the complete subjectivity achieved in philosophy is re-objectified. Art is a strong candidate to take the post-philosophy position. In fact, many works of art are not explained only through 'thought'. In addition, Hegel himself knows better than anyone else who has had a wealth of artistic experiences. For this reason, this discrepancy between the method and the philosophical system is all the more regrettable.

When we lived in shipping container whilst building our home we ended up putting a huge skillion roof over our containers. It reduced the heat buy about 5c in summer and stopped condensation in the winter. Also enormously increased our water catchment area which kept our 3 small tanks full (1321 gallons) & our 28,000 gallon tank half filled.

Yeah, water is a primary life issue. I believe I suggested more water storage some time ago. I used to plan out sufficient water needs. A common way is to figure out what you need on an average day, figure in the most realistic water uses above daily needs, throw in normal weather shortage issues and then double or triple your water storage capability (if you have water storage charging capacity). With added capacity,  you can also feature the capability to add a fire hose feature. So, can you add double or triple storage? Water is not wasted, it allows more uses. I think you have found that batteries also degrade faster when when you allow them to discharge lower that about 20% capacity. You can add the ability to warn your phone as it reaches programed levels. To address this issues, my bro-in-law got a great deal on batteries (problems with some cells and had ALL of them replaced by the company. The company told him to send the old ones back. He said, NOT IN CONTRACT, COME GET THEM. So, he already had the new bank and replaced two new batteries. Now he has two battery banks and has sufficient panels and computerized support to keep them all charged. Good luck.

I wonder if it would make sense to put in two service panels in the beginning and split your loads between them. You could start by feeding both with the old battery bank and then when you upgrade you could add 1/2 a new bank and power one panel from the old and the other panel from the new. Then later you can replace the old. That way you will only have 1/2 the cost for upgrades every few years. You will also be able to take advantage of tech improvements in small increments. The cost of a second panel would not be that much for the benefits of spreading costs over time.
Batteries are always the issue. You should have more batteries than you need. Also, a good-sized generator will power up those batteries when needed. You don't need to fully recharge the batteries with the generator. Just put in enough to get you through the night and the sun will charge them up the next day.  You have a good-sized pond there, I'd be using that water to keep the lawns and gardens green. God bless you both.

Ya know, ya'll are brave and I salute your gallant efforts and all, but, I would seriously consider making a disclaimer once in awhile. Everything, I mean everything, thanks to inflation, is EXPENSIVE.  And unfortunately, with recent irresponsible legislation passed in D.C., anything GREEN is gonna cost much more, and along with that, more suffocating regulatory rules will be added to.  So, going off-grid relying totally on solar is gonna become a much tougher alternative.  So, not to be a bummer or anything, living on the grid isn't really such a bad thing. Despite what you hear, read, etc. about the planet, we are not killing it. Fear mongers want us to believe that nonsense but common sense should tell you otherwise. We have plenty of fresh, breathable air and plenty of clean water to last thousands of years.  Do we need to be good stewards of what God gave us? Duh!  Another thing, reliance on fossil fuels isn't going away, regardless of what activist environmentalists may want.  Wind & solar are not sustainable sources, sorry, that's just the truth.  They are ok as alternative sources but they will NEVER, EVER replace Nat gas, oil, nuclear, and hydro. Your issues prove it out, especially if you apply what you have experienced and apply those kinds of scenarios to much, much larger scales (nationally or globally).  If you are having to oversize your panel array and your increase your battery supply, think about what would be required to totally rely on solar and/or wind to totally power the nation-wide grid.  It will NEVER happen because it is IMPOSSIBLE.  The sun is not a constant, nor is the wind.  Therefore, it can NOT be done. It is also financially unsustainable. The sooner everyone accepts these FACTS, the better.  Now, I hope & pray you are able to live your lives the way you want to, on or off the grid or whatever works. I think you would be wise to put that out there, not to discourage people from going off grid but just to forewarn them about what they may be up against.  That just makes sense to me.  Take care and be safe.

Spencer, you need to look into those lithium ion phosphate batteries. Iâ€™m guessing you are using recycled lead acid which should not be discharged below 50% and have a 5-6 year life expectancy. These newer ones last 10 years or longer and for many thousand deeper cycles. Check out the Red Poppy Ranch YouTuber up in Idaho. He screwed around with lead acid for a couple years before moving to these lithium ion phosphate ones and he says they are a night and day improvement. Keep them in the house if you have room. They retain their efficiency much longer in moderate temps.   Really like your vidâ€™s. Keep them coming. I envy your stamina but recognize you are about 50 years younger. I have no idea if you'll ever see this comment. But I lived in Sacramento my little 60-year-old house had no insulation in the walls or the ceiling I guess you call it a ceiling is above the structure anyway. I painted the outside of my home with radiant barrier additive to the paint then later we were able to put some kind of radiant barrier in the Attic area which those guys had such a hard time getting it in because of the way the previous owner had left the upstairs attic area I mean they had to go through the crawl hole in the ceiling. Once we did that remember there's no insulation I didn't have any insulation put down in the Attic nor in the walls it dropped the temperature from 130Â° down to 85. Get radiant barrier foil get it in the 250 sheet roll if you can but if you can't do that then you get the additive and repaint every wall inside your house and outside and you will not have to use that AC so often.

By being a container home you have no real thermal mass like a concrete slab which is great for regulating temp inside the house. Another option is running a circuit of downpipe, probably 4' below ground or wherever the soil feels cool. Having a fan that sucks the hot air out of the high point of your house running it through say 30-40yards of pipe will exchange the heat into the ground and return cooler air to the lower areas of your house. Reducing the electrical load on your solar system.

With your abundant electricity during the day maybe consider storing up cold energy. Use your electricity to make ice and store it in a cold well. You can make a YouTube project out of it. Alexander The Great had cold Wells made in his era to provide ice and probably cold food storage into spring (or so depending on area and construction). I read articles years ago about AC units that would be made to do something like that during the night (build up ice do the next day). I'm about 6 years offgrid and 1 week ago I replaced my batteries again. I downgraded in amp hours, but got batteries that don't self discharge as fast. I just got some Renogy gel cells. I haven't used my ac in over 2 years now. 2 summers. I have a generator (and fresh gas stored), but haven't used it. I'm getting by with utilizing house shading, opening windows at the right time and fans. 2 new Kobalt 24V fans are blowing on me now, as a window fan blows cooler night air into the house. And lots of showers too! But I can see now signs of summer waining this year and I rejoice! Another idea, besides shading your house better, would be to store all that cold energy you use already by making a thermal mass in your house. So you can make a brick wall in your house or wall and water tanks, or combination. This would also help in winter to keep it warm. As long as it stays "charged" and you don't let the extremes inside for it to be hard to "charge" back up... Another great thermal masses to make a large fireplace of stone, brick, cement, etc... It still hold cold energy in summer from the AC running on your abundant daytime energy.  Good luck!
Hot-tip for working in the hot sun: Cover up. Broad-brim hat, long sleeve shirt and trousers. Counter-intuitive, but keeping the sun off your bare skin is the key to powering on all day. (As is staying hydrated!)

If your batteries are getting "full" while the sun is still high and the AC units are cycling you might look into "super cooling" your house.  Turn the thermostat down so that it the AC runs all the time once the batteries are full.  Then when the panels quit making power turn it back up to your normal setting.  It may be a couple hours (hopefully) before the AC runs again.  This is used in the opposite way to cut down on electric bills by super cooling a house at night when it is cooler out and one is off of peak demand.  It seems logical but who knows.  Please let us know if it works so we can keep it in mind once we are off grid.

Hi from Canada... have watched and enjoyed your channel for a long time!  Have been thinking about your water situation and your possible use of your pond area.  What about a project where you filter your pond water (via a sand pump) as suggested by another viewer.  The filtered water would go into a collection basin where it is hit by swimming pool bleaching pucks then pumped into a water tower set up.  This could be done gradually throughout the year until you are topped up.  You could also keeping topping it up as the drought season continues.  That should help with your water capacity issues.  the water tower would be used for washing, showering, cleaning etc., to extend your existing potable water supply.  Might make for a cool project moving forward.  Your area of Texas seems to have plenty of sunshine and wind for renewable energy gathering.  What about a power wall option for storage?  You have probably considered these ideas!
Rather than hauling in the water yourself, reach out to your local dairies.  A lot of them supplement the cost of their milk trucks by using them to haul potable water.  My family used them to fill pools when they were in the pool business, and could get 2k gallons on site for about $200, without the wear and tear on your little hauler.

I can't remember where I heard it, but one who had been using solar for several years were very clear about making sure to have batteries to last you through several days of bad weather with nominal use. If you burn through over night, then it really sound like you have a battery system that's less than a 3rd of what you actually need..  It actually bugs me that I can't remember where I heard it, but the suggesting was that you should figured out you normal use and make sure you have batteries to keep that up for 3-5 days without any solar power coming in. You should also look at how much power your solar system is making at the best days. If they currently can fill up your batteries within a few hours, they yes you have way to little battery system, doubling that up should last you through the night and then some more and still get fully filled up on a good day.
Thats one thing I have noticed on all of the solar discussions is that everyone focuses on the solar system and the more important battery storage seems to be an after thought.

Have you considered micro hydro? You may not have a suitable stream to produce power, but if you do have excess solar electricity, you could use the excess power to pump water to an elevated storage tank that you can release through a micro hydro system for an on demand power back-up 'bank.'  Much like the windmill powered stock tanks in the American west, the pump doesn't have to move large quantities of water, so long as it runs all day, every day whenever it's sunny. The water in the system would be recycled within this system, but if maintained, it could also be a potable water source as well. Freezing might be an issue, but the thermal mass of a full tank should give overnight freeze protection and much like roof mounted solar hot water heaters the lines could be purged to the resting water storage if needed.  Just a thought.

Over 60% of Texas is in D3 drought pushing D4 which is as high as the scale goes as far as I know.  My main water line has already busted twice this month from the ground opening up.
So the first thing to do is switch off the AC at night. Open your windows and all your internal doors. Use a fan (I use 24 inch) placed at the bottom of your bed and the same for the little ones. The fans along with a spray bottle full of water will keep you cool. In fact at times I have been cold after I misted myself with water. Its a lot easer on the batteries just using fans and cold water. I live in Cyprus where the temps get up to what you have had this year every year. We have no rain from May until October, November or even December. June, July and August have been in the 40's and have not used the AC once as the fans and a spray bottle works just as well during the day and of course all the windows are shut with thick curtains at the windows to keep the heat out. During the day a damp cloth (T towel or a T-shirt or similar) wrapped around your neck will cool you down even more. Thanks for posting

Grain of salt I am not an expert!! this is what I would do  First you need an automatic backup generator. batteries 10% gen kicks on charges to say 50% ?  Next. ultimate? you want 400% more battery capacity. you want 2 full days of power in case "something breaks" on the solar side you now have 2 days to fix 4 days if you cut out HVAC  OR with a backup generator? double your batt capacity and segregate. Second charge controller. use the old battery pack JUST for the HVAC and nothing else. use the new battery pack for the "rest of the house"  this will do 2 things. one double your battery run time for hvac (probably 4 or 5 times for everything else) and if the HVAC does drain the batts the rest of the house will keep working fine on its own battery pack.
And since your containers get real hot, the AC works that much harder...The solar panels will last you about 25 years and then you will have a problem getting rid of them because they will leak poisons. I love my lithium phosphate batteries.   It has really improved life in the jungle.  But, I didn't realize I could actually run my minisplit during the night.  I tried to buy a third lithium battery...but they are discontinued.  Buy BIG!  I have a big generator that I've been using to top off the batteries before bedtime( we have big storms in the afternoons that limit pm solar sun).  Consider all sleeping in one room and only using one minisplit in the meantime.  Also, the minisplit use considerably less energy in the dehumidify mode.
Go watch Todd at 1870 homestead. He has found two larger that work great. You need a backup to offset what you are doing so they can be backup eventually. Search off grid cabin video. He has used two others on the homestead as well. His wife handles disaster relief for power for a living as a project manager. The water you might have to haul since you don't have much snow to collect. We had to do the same. Don't let tanks get below 100 gallons to avoid pump burnouts.

f you had a way to vent out the heat inside, during the evening, the A/C would not have to work as hard.  Hot air rises, and air does compress.  This air is 'packing into the upper area of your fantastic home.  As air is heated it expands, and if the venting (natural leaks around doors and windows) the cool air is being forced out and has to be continually replaced.  If you got rid of the heated air, the cool air would not have to be replaced as much, and the A/C would not have to work as hard.  A thermostatically controlled vent (or two) placed in the high center, would probably decrease the nighttime temperatures by about 10 degrees.  Just a thought.

10:04 That looks like a broad-banded water snake. Generally harmless and usually found in the southeast from Louisiana to North Carolina, I don't know how common they are in Texas but, I would imagine they would be, especially in the swamps of south Texas and around the gulf coast. They will instinctually strike if threatened (though they don't have fangs, they can break the skin and draw blood, and it doesn't feel pleasant) and they produce a strong-smelling musky oil to repel predators and defend themselves, but are non-venomous and generally no threat to humans unless a bite breaks the skin and gets infected. Like any reptile, they have lots of bacteria in their mouths that could make small children and animals sick.  They are often mistaken for copperheads because of their red, brown, and black coloring, and sometimes mistaken for water mokisons/cottonmouths because they are usually seen near, and spend over two-thirds of their time in or around, water. They are very good swimmers, and will usually just run away if they are spooked or feel threatened. As they get older those brighter colors tend to darken and become less vivid and dull over time. That one has a particularly interesting coloration pattern up around its neck and head, and is quite a beautiful specimen. You got some really good footage of that very cool snake.

You should look into the Micro-Air easy start for your A/C. EasyStart allows the starting of your air conditioner on low voltage power sources. IT electronically reduces the high power start-up current needed to START your AC, and permits to both START and RUN you AC at the much lower voltage current needed to RUN it.  This should help with the high amp draw with both starting and running.

Totally agree with the book idea.  So many people would benefit from it and you could make a tidy profit.  Your new snakes are copperheads.  They are poisonous, but usually not deadly. The darker ones you are accustomed to seeing could be water moccasins (aka cottonmouths). The cottonmouth has a powerful cytotoxic venom that destroys tissue and is considered more toxic than that of the Copperhead. The bite symptoms commonly include severe pain, swelling, ecchymosis, and even though deaths are rare, the cottonmouth bite could leave scars and on occasion lead to amputation. I have noticed that you relocated snakes thus giving the snakes the opportunity to 1. make more snakes and 2. come back to your home.  When I was growing up in Texas, we killed them.  Eventually, we reduced the snake population around our home.  The rodent population didn't not explode.  Perhaps that's because we had domestic dogs and cats.

When my disabled amputee farmer stepdad built our 4bdrm 2 1/2 bath farmhouse in 1976 we never had A/C. First he dug out a basement so in the hot summers it stayed temperate and cool and winters it was fairly warm. We had two fireplaces.. one upstairs and the other in the basement. Dad modified a smaller iron wood stove making it larger for the downstairs fireplace and rigged a professional exhaust fan above it with ducting to keep the upstairs warm in the fall/winter months. Additionally, he built a large attic that took away the heat from the upstairs and vented it on either sides. Also with a pitched rambler style roofline he had 2 1/2 - 3ft overhangs over all the windows. In the shade it's instantly 10 degrees cooler. Combined with many large bay screened split pane windows it was easy to get cool breezes into the home. We had one box fan in the living room to help circulate the air. All these planned measures kept the electric bill for the whole house under $100..something today might be over $300-$500 monthly. I've managed in my 559 sq. ft. apartment to lower my electric bill by upsetting my A/C from 73 in past years to 78/79 degrees if it's over 80 degrees outside at night.

Great job guys! The house looks awesome!
I feel your pain.  We were always running out of water when we were young.  Our summers are hot and long here too. While we are not entirely off grid, we are responsible for our own water supply.  This is what we learned.  Anything you can collect from - sheds, carports - anything - gets sent to the watertanks.  You can never have too many of those either.  Try and have a secondary source as well.  Dams, creeks, well, deep lead bore - this is helpful for irrigating plants, flushing toilets and cleaning up outside.  We plumbed our house for rainwater for drinking and cooking only, and everything else comes from a deep lead bore as we have no access to creeks or rivers.  Once we put the bore in, we've never run out of water and that's with 8 of us here.  We have 3 massive tanks on a hill above our house so our water is gravity fed to the house.  Cuts power use as only pump to fill the tanks from the bore or the holding watertank. Green lawns limit the risk of fire too, and also help keep the dust down so it doesn't get in your water collection system, cake your solar panels or fill your house.  Love your channel, it's brilliant - and yes, write that book or you will regret not doing it later.

I have an idea to help the AC both day and night. Run some 1/2" tubing from the pond with using a small pump to spray water on the AC condenser units. This will help the AC work less. Control the pump motor so it only turns on when the condenser fan is running. You will want to filter the intake at the pond to keep debris from entering the system. You can use store bought spray nozzles or just put tiny holes in the tubing to spray on the coils. There are some store bought units available but most don't have any pumps. Hope this will help you.

If you do a second set of batteries set yourself up as a dual switching system I believe you would be better off...   I did one in my RV. I charge 1 set of batteries while the other set is being used. I use a power relay that is on when that set is being used and turns off the relay when a current limit threshold is set to specific volt (voltage comparator circuitry). I have 2 different sets of aged batteries running with 4+ years age difference. So I am not out the thousand plus for the 8 batteries, only half at a time. I also have a secondary system charge set up too. The secondary system is for battery equalization charge - this is on the solar charge system set up. So I charge the deep cycle batteries like every 4-6 times to have the equalization charge being done, lead acid and gel batteries. If you do Lithium Iron Phosphate (Lipo4) there is a BMS - battery management system built into each separate battery built, a high initial cost but will last longer in the right environment (BMS equalize the charge every time). I live in the cold North - Minnesota (Father use to live in TX) so I do not want to build a heater and cooling system into my battery storage areas. And then have the batteries run those systems too (EV Cars have that built in). I have both lead acid and gel. With a dual or triple battery set up you can even do the (1) charge cycle, (2) cool down cycle and (3) the usable cycle rotation setups. That is my next goal and will be with a plug in setup for the next set of batteries to be in the trailer/boat... If you know of someone who can do a program you could do an Arduino  (mini PC) controller program to watch the usage and control on the system and maybe add a temp sensor to the mix so not over heat the batteries in both charge and run states.. There is a fair amount of variations and choices you can do or choose to do. I run several ganged 200 amp relays to do a single battery switching I also did a lag turn off circuit so not to interrupt the power switching. Additionally I took a 1 wire GM self exciting alternator and have a DuroMax electric start 18 HP motor running to charge up the batts as an aux charge.  Mine is the 12v 110 amp self exciter alternator from Amazon. I generally start the motor to a max 1800 rpm to dump a base charge into the batts and then let it run at idle for the next 12 hours or so on its own 1 tank full of gas. - YES 12 hours on the tank of gas filled to the top of the tank... (I run only 600 watt solar system on the RV) good luck!!!   Um Oh for different voltage rating relays check out the electrical industrial trucks and get the amp load you need. I cheated with 12v 200 amp relays my system is only 12v not a 24 or larger. (I use to manage 1400 deep cycle industrial batteries and charging for 6 years... reach truck, sit down, walkie rider, and the gulf cart like machines in a warehouse.) I know you probably hate people making suggestions to you, but I'm gonna take a chance anyway. I suggest figuring out a way to shade your HVAC condensers in the summer so they don't work so hard. I understand why you might not want to plant a tree, which would provide shade in the summer and lose it's leaves to allow the sunshine to hit the condensers in the winter--there are maintenance factors as well as the leaves shedding close to the equipment. However, perhaps you could rig up some beautiful sun shade sails in some way to add a design/feature element, but also allows the flexibility of removing it in the winter to allow the sun to reach them. I know that I save a lot of money/power usage by having some smaller shade trees casting a shadow on my HVAC for most of the hot summer days.  Whatever you decide...good luck! I believe in you!!

Have you guys considered using the pond as a geothermal heatsink?   You can take coils of piping and place them in the bottom of the pond then run water through it that circulates under the flooring to help cool (or heat) the house.   A circulating pump is the only power needed but the cooling effect will reduce the split units usage well lower than what the pump uses.   Just a thought.

My 4th time saying this. Drive a point 10 ft into the ground. The level of your pond is the level of water in the ground. The ground is the best natural holding tank for rain you have. The channel Technology Connections suggests a counter intuitive way of storing some of that daytime energy (video titled "Batteries aren't the only way to store energy. Here's another.").  We're all trained to turn the thermostat up during the day and down at night to not overload the grid, but y'all don't have that grid-overloading problem.  You have a storage problem.  So, you can turn your thermostats down during the day when there is a surplus of energy, and use the insulated space as a "battery" to store that energy by way of cooler temps.  At night, your AC units will have less work to keep an already cool space cool, and you can turn the thermostats up to make it even easier on them (and your batteries).
Would fans draw less power than the AC?  If so, you might want to consider using them at night.

Because of the energy draw and that you live in a dry climate, you should think about supplementing you cooling with a swamp cooler. I use one in northern California (currently 100+ decrees) and don't need an air conditioner. My summer energy bills are low because of it. This is why grid-scale green energy just isn't ready yet.  There isn't a practical way to store energy to account for demand versus unpredictable generation.  Batteries have limited lifespans and require large amounts of energy and rare-earth materials to manufacture.  It's fine for a single house, but not at scales equal to the power grid.

Here are some suggestions.... You have access to grid power on your property, use it! For off gridders there is no shame in going with a grid tied system. The grid will fill in for the times when there isn't enough solar or battery power. You can also back feed the grid when you have excess power and get credit for it. So in the end your net power usage from the grid is still zero. As for water... Drill a well as soon as the budget allows. wells are off grid!
I think that you will find that installing a well for back up use will give you some of the independence that you desire as well as allowing you to water and irrigate as needed.

My parents and I would always shut off the livingroom and all sleep in that one room when it got hot enough to use the A/C instead of cooling all rooms.  There may have been only three of us, but that savings helped.  When I was raising my young son, I would move his twin bed into my bedroom and do the same, put up an asian 3 fold screen for privacy for him when he got older.  In the morning we would use the fans to bring in the cool air to the entire apartment.  I could barely make ends meet and, with food donation banks to help with meals and necessities, this really helped.  A LOT.  Perhaps you could do the same with very small beds or sleeping bags/playpen for the kids on foam bases for comfort.  Or raise the A/C 5 degrees.  Just a thought.

When compared with AGM lead-acid batteries, the primary advantages of lithium-ion are efficiency-based, especially the ability to be charged very rapidly, several times faster than AGM chemistry. One of my very few comments on this channel was right after the solar system expansion, and that was that it was time for additional batteries.
This is just another Texas summer. The heat and drought is just part of the cycle. I have lived in Texas all my life and have seen it just like this many times.
I've been living off-grid for the past 11 years. I've had to upgrade my solar system severely to accommodate my needs. I finally drilled a deep well 600ft and due to 7gpm I also had to bury a 2500gal storage tank to pump water into. This necessitated the installation of jet pump to pump water into a pressure tank to give me 24/7 water. I have a dual electric/propane water heater, electric refrigerator and deep freezer. Batteries are the biggest piece of an off-grid solar system. I purchased Toshiba LTO SCIB batteries from a junk yard from two Honda Fit EV wrecked vehicles for $4K that give me 44kW of battery bank storage. I can run my place for almost 5 days and nights without any sunshine however, I have a propane generator attached to my system that can recharge the batteries to full charge in 6 hours. I wouldn't think that you need to go very deep for a well with all the surface water around your property and you now can get almost the same amount of battery storage I have by purchasing server rack batteries for less than $1,500 per 5kW of storage for Lipo4 batteries, which are safe batteries. I don't know what you are currently using but, I think that you can integrate your existing batteries with the new ones.

If you decide to upgrade your batteries I recommend server batteries. They'll run a 48 volt inverter also you can rack mount them. Most are 3kv @ 100A. You can buy a bunch of them, rack Mount them. Although I recommend using a external buss bar for the battery connections and not daisy chaining them. When you daisy chain them you can heat up the jumper wire between the batteries and the internal buss bar under heavy prolonged loads. Using a external bus bar it keeps the jumper wire cooler. It is expandable. Keep your old battery bank for backup or to power the barn when that project starts.

Off-grid tip: many adapters do indeed underestimate even their current power needs. Remember, we were brought up to just "turn up the tap" if we needed more juice. Just cannot do this as easily (or inexpensively) with an off-grid system.  As those kids grow into "wasteful" young-lings, power (and water) requirements will skyrocket. Despite the added upfront expense, over-building capacity is always a good idea. Doubtful people will complain about having "too much" output.

That electric storage space could stand a Rammed Earth walls surrounding the space for maintaining a cooler environment. Frost line thick walls would mean the area inside the 4 walls could remain 65ish degrees if the roof and floor were insulated properly. Wouldn't take much to maintain it with that heavy of heat load. Definitely wouldn't have to worry as much in the winter time. A few 4inch pipes buried 3to4 foot deep and 50 feet out would transfer cool air if you wanted to keep the same space just looking for a natural way to heat and cool a space. Same could be done with an enclosure for the water tanks. Then the roof of the tanks storage unit help with rainfall harvest. Another big win

Climate Change is making weather events more extreme. This will impact the worlds food supply.   Floods, droughts, fires, excessive heat and low water levels in reservoirs and in ground water storage are becoming more common. We must stop adding more fossil fuels to the climate fire. India, China, Brazil, Japan, US, Europe, Indonesia, Canada, Germany and Australia  need to do more to reduce use of fossil fuels. They must stop building and financing more coal fossil fuels power plants.  Time to go all in on wind and solar energy and electric vehicles.  Fossil fuels are a bad investment for people and the planet. Investors are divesting from fossil fuels. ESG investing is growing. The cost of dealing with the impact of climate change will be greater than the cost of reducing the impact. The world population is expected to grow by another billion people. Where will the food come from ?

If "solar power" is insufficient for the dark hours....then there's a question as to whether it can be relied on for the country....and we're being sold a bill of goods.....which I think we are.
Put the AC on a timer to shut off at 2am. You're sleeping and you really can be warmer while you are all unconscious. It's a myth that cooling off after warming up takes more energy. It actually takes the exact same amount as 24-hour climate control. 'Kenz', you nailed it, "it's not good to keep draining the batteries down to zero." I hope this energy-saving (and battery saving) tip helps, cheers!
